					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Language spoken in Hokkaido, Japan</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the language spoken on Hokkaido. For the language family, see <a href="/wiki/Ainu_languages" title="Ainu languages">Ainu languages</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/%C3%84ynu_language" title="Äynu language">Äynu language</a>.</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox vevent"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above above" style="font-size:125%; color: black; background-color: darkseagreen;">Hokkaido Ainu</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-subheader" style="font-size:110%; color: black; background-color: darkseagreen;"><span title="Ainu (Japan)-language text"><span lang="ain">アイヌ・イタㇰ</span></span> <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn">Ainu-itak</i></span></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-image"><span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Frameless"><a href="/wiki/File:Multilingual_sign_at_Ainu_Museum_(Shiraoi).JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="A multilingual exit sign." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Multilingual_sign_at_Ainu_Museum_%28Shiraoi%29.JPG/200px-Multilingual_sign_at_Ainu_Museum_%28Shiraoi%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Multilingual_sign_at_Ainu_Museum_%28Shiraoi%29.JPG/300px-Multilingual_sign_at_Ainu_Museum_%28Shiraoi%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Multilingual_sign_at_Ainu_Museum_%28Shiraoi%29.JPG/400px-Multilingual_sign_at_Ainu_Museum_%28Shiraoi%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a></span><div class="infobox-caption" style="padding:0.35em 0.35em 0.25em;line-height:1.25em;">Multilingual sign in <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>, Ainu, <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Written_Chinese" title="Written Chinese">Chinese</a>. The Ainu text, in <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a>, is second down from the top on the right side of the sign. It reads <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language text"><span lang="ain">イヤイライケㇾ</span></span> (<span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn">iyairaiker</i></span>), meaning "thank you".</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;">Pronunciation</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="ain-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">&#91;ˈainu<span class="wrap"> </span>iˈtak&#93;</a></span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;">Native&#160;to</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;">Region</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;">Ethnicity</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;">25,000 (1986) to ca. 200,000 (no date) <a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu people</a><sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><div style="display: inline-block; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /></video></span><figcaption>An Ainu speaker, recorded in Japan</figcaption></figure>
<p><b>Ainu</b> (<span title="Ainu (Japan)-language text"><span lang="ain">アイヌ・イタㇰ</span></span>, <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn">Ainu-itak</i></span>), or more precisely <b>Hokkaido Ainu</b> (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">北海道アイヌ語</span>), is a language spoken by a few elderly members of the <a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu people</a> on the northern Japanese island of <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>. It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Ainu_languages" title="Ainu languages">Ainu language family</a>, itself considered a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language family isolate</a> with no academic consensus of origin. It is classified as Critically Endangered by the <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/Atlas_of_the_World%27s_Languages_in_Danger" title="Atlas of the World&#39;s Languages in Danger">Atlas of the World's Languages in Danger</a>.
</p><p>Until the 20th century, the Ainu languages – Hokkaido Ainu and the now-extinct <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Ainu_language" title="Kuril Ainu language">Kuril Ainu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin_Ainu_language" title="Sakhalin Ainu language">Sakhalin Ainu</a> – were spoken throughout Hokkaido, the southern half of the island of <a href="/wiki/Ainu_in_Russia" title="Ainu in Russia">Sakhalin</a> and by small numbers of people in the <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands" title="Kuril Islands">Kuril Islands</a>. Due to the colonization policy employed by the Japanese government, the number of Hokkaido Ainu speakers decreased through the 20th century, and it is now <a href="/wiki/Moribund_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Moribund language">moribund</a>. A very low number of elderly people still speak the language fluently, though attempts are being made to revive it.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Speakers">Speakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Speakers"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Pirka_Kotan,_Sapporo.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The entrance to the carpark of the Pirka Kotan Museum." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Pirka_Kotan%2C_Sapporo.JPG/220px-Pirka_Kotan%2C_Sapporo.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Pirka_Kotan%2C_Sapporo.JPG/330px-Pirka_Kotan%2C_Sapporo.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Pirka_Kotan%2C_Sapporo.JPG/440px-Pirka_Kotan%2C_Sapporo.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption>Pirka Kotan Museum, an Ainu language and cultural center in <a href="/wiki/Sapporo" title="Sapporo">Sapporo</a> (Jozankei area)</figcaption></figure>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>, Ainu is an <a href="/wiki/Endangered_language" title="Endangered language">endangered language</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Martin2011_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin2011-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> with few native speakers amongst the country's approximately 30,000 Ainu people,<sup id="cite_ref-gayman2012_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gayman2012-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> a number that may be higher due to a potentially low rate of self-identification as Ainu within the country's ethnic Ainu population.<sup id="cite_ref-Okazaki,_T_2011_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Okazaki,_T_2011-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Knowledge of the language, which has been endangered since before the 1960s, has declined steadily since; in 2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, just 304 people within Japan were reported to understand the Ainu language to some extent.<sup id="cite_ref-Okazaki,_T_2011_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Okazaki,_T_2011-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, <i>Ethnologue</i> has listed Ainu as class 8b, "nearly extinct".<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A survey of the Ainu people's life was done by the Hokkaido government in 2017, and about 671 people participated in it.<sup id="cite_ref-https&#58;//www.pref.hokkaido.lg.jp/ks/ass/new_jittai.html_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-https://www.pref.hokkaido.lg.jp/ks/ass/new_jittai.html-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The participants were those who were believed to be descendants of Ainu or who joined Ainu families by marriage or adoption.<sup id="cite_ref-https&#58;//www.pref.hokkaido.lg.jp/ks/ass/new_jittai.html_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-https://www.pref.hokkaido.lg.jp/ks/ass/new_jittai.html-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The topic of the survey included the Ainu language, and in regard to fluency, 0.7% of participants answered that they would "be able to have a conversation" in the Ainu language, 3.4% answered that they would "be able to have a conversation a little," 44.6% answered they would "not be able to have a conversation but have a little knowledge of the Ainu language," and 48.1% answered that they would "not be able to have a conversation or understand the language by listening".<sup id="cite_ref-https&#58;//www.pref.hokkaido.lg.jp/ks/ass/new_jittai.html_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-https://www.pref.hokkaido.lg.jp/ks/ass/new_jittai.html-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The survey was done in 2006 and 2013 as well, and by comparing those with the 2017 survey, notable trends were observed: the percentage of people who answered  they would "be able to have a conversation in the Ainu language" declined in the age 60s group (2.3% in 2006, 1.9% in 2013, and 0.4% in 2017), but increased in the age 30s group (0% in 2006, 0% in 2013, and 2.3% in 2017).<sup id="cite_ref-https&#58;//www.pref.hokkaido.lg.jp/ks/ass/new_jittai.html_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-https://www.pref.hokkaido.lg.jp/ks/ass/new_jittai.html-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> However, there was little change overall (0.7% in 2006, 0.9% in 2013, and 0.7% in 2017).<sup id="cite_ref-https&#58;//www.pref.hokkaido.lg.jp/ks/ass/new_jittai.html_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-https://www.pref.hokkaido.lg.jp/ks/ass/new_jittai.html-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_recognition">Official recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Official recognition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Japan" title="Government of Japan">Japanese government</a> made a decision to recognize Ainu as an <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_language" title="Indigenous language">indigenous language</a> in June 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Martin2011_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin2011-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The Japanese government approved and passed a <a href="/wiki/Bill_(law)" title="Bill (law)">bill</a> officially recognising the indigeneity of the <a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu people</a> in  2019.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 12 July 2020, the Japanese government opened the <a href="/wiki/National_Ainu_Museum" title="National Ainu Museum">National Ainu Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shiraoi,_Hokkaido" title="Shiraoi, Hokkaido">Shiraoi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> It forms one of three institutions named Upopoy (which means 'singing in a large group' in the Ainu language) alongside the National Ainu Park and a memorial site on high ground on the east side of Lake Poroto (ポロト湖) where Ainu services are held. Its director, Masahiro Nomoto, says that "One of our main objectives is to preserve and revive the language, as this is one of the most threatened elements of <a href="/wiki/Ainu_culture" title="Ainu culture">Ainu culture</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Announcements on some bus routes in Hokkaido can since be heard in Ainu, efforts are being undertaken to archive Ainu speech recordings by the <a href="/wiki/Agency_for_Cultural_Affairs" title="Agency for Cultural Affairs">Agency for Cultural Affairs</a>, and there is a popular educational <a href="/wiki/Youtube_channel" class="mw-redirect" title="Youtube channel">YouTube channel</a> which teaches conversational Ainu.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While these measures have been praised for taking steps to protect the Ainu language and culture, the museum and related government efforts have been criticised for failing to acknowledge the history of Japanese discrimination against the Ainu people, and for the government's refusal to apologise for past misdeeds against the Ainu.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Phonology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Ainu <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllables</a> are (C)V(C); they have an obligatory vowel, and an optional <a href="/wiki/Syllable_onset" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllable onset">syllable onset</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syllable_coda" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllable coda">coda</a> consisting of one consonant. There are few <a href="/wiki/Consonant_cluster" title="Consonant cluster">consonant clusters</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Vowels"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p><a href="/wiki/Obstruent" title="Obstruent">Obstruents</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/p<span class="wrap"> </span>t<span class="wrap"> </span>ts~tʃ<span class="wrap"> </span>k/</span> may be voiced <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[b<span class="wrap"> </span>d<span class="wrap"> </span>dz~dʒ<span class="wrap"> </span>ɡ]</span> between vowels and after nasals. <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t͡s/</span> can be heard as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[t͡ʃ]</span> in free variation among speakers. Both <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ti/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/tsi/</span> are realized as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[t͡ʃi]</span>, and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/s/</span> becomes <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʃ]</span> before <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span> and at the end of syllables. <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/h/</span> is heard as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɸ]</span> when occurring before <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span>. <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/n/</span> is heard as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ŋ]</span> when before <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/k/</span>, as well as in final position. A glottal stop <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʔ]</span> is often inserted at the beginning of words, before an accented vowel, but is non-phonemic.
</p><p>The Ainu language also has a <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" class="mw-redirect" title="Pitch accent">pitch accent</a> system. Generally, words containing <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">affixes</a> have a high pitch on a syllable in the stem. This will typically fall on the first syllable if that is long (has a final consonant or a diphthong), and will otherwise fall on the second syllable, though there are exceptions to this generalization.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Typology_and_grammar">Typology and grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Typology and grammar"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Linguistic_typology" title="Linguistic typology">Typologically</a>, Ainu is similar in word order (and some aspects of phonology) to <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>.
</p><p>Ainu has a canonical word order of <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb" class="mw-redirect" title="Subject–object–verb">subject, object, verb</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-wals.info_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wals.info-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> and uses postpositions rather than <a href="/wiki/Preposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Preposition">prepositions</a>. <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">Nouns</a> can cluster to modify one another; the head comes at the end. Verbs, which are inherently either <a href="/wiki/Transitivity_(grammatical_category)" class="mw-redirect" title="Transitivity (grammatical category)">transitive</a> or intransitive, accept various <a href="/wiki/Derivation_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Derivation (linguistics)">derivational affixes</a>. Ainu does not have <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">grammatical gender</a>. Plurals are indicated by a suffix.<sup id="cite_ref-wals.info_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wals.info-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Classical Ainu, the language of the <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Yukar" title="Yukar">yukar</a></i></span>, is <a href="/wiki/Polysynthetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Polysynthetic">polysynthetic</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Incorporation_(linguistics)" title="Incorporation (linguistics)">incorporation</a> of nouns and adverbs; this is greatly reduced in the modern colloquial language.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Applicative_voice" title="Applicative voice">Applicatives</a> may be used in Ainu to place nouns in <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_case" title="Instrumental case">instrumental</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comitative_case" title="Comitative case">comitative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Locative_case" title="Locative case">locative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allative_case" title="Allative case">allative</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Ablative_case" title="Ablative case">ablative</a> roles. Besides freestanding nouns, these roles may be assigned to incorporated nouns, and such use of applicatives is in fact mandatory for incorporating <a href="/wiki/Oblique_case" title="Oblique case">oblique</a> nouns. Like incorporation, applicatives have grown less common in the modern language.
</p><p>Ainu has a closed class of <a href="/wiki/Plural_verb" class="mw-redirect" title="Plural verb">plural verbs</a>, and some of these are <a href="/wiki/Suppletion" title="Suppletion">suppletive</a>.
</p><p>Ainu has a system of verbal affixes (shown below) which mark agreement for person and case. The specific cases that are marked differ by person, with <a href="/wiki/Nominative%E2%80%93accusative_alignment" title="Nominative–accusative alignment">nominative–accusative marking</a> for the first person singular, <a href="/wiki/Tripartite_alignment" title="Tripartite alignment">tripartite marking</a> for the first person plural and <a href="/wiki/Indefinite_pronoun" title="Indefinite pronoun">indefinite (or 'fourth')</a> person, and <a href="/wiki/Direct_alignment" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct alignment">direct or 'neutral' marking</a> for the second singular and plural, and third persons (i.e. the affixes do not differ by case).<sup id="cite_ref-SOAS_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SOAS-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MalchukovComrie2015_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalchukovComrie2015-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center">

</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sentence_types">Sentence types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Sentence types"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Intransitive_sentences">Intransitive sentences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Intransitive sentences"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:4em"><div style="position:absolute;left:1em">a.</div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">クアニ</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">Kuani</p><p style="margin: 0px;">I</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">クイタㇰ。</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ku-itak.</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="first person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">1SG</abbr>-speak</p></div><p style="display: none;">クアニ クイタㇰ。</p><p style="display: none;">Kuani ku-itak.</p><p style="display: none;">I 1SG-speak</p><p style="clear: left;">'I spoke.'<sup id="cite_ref-Shibatani_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shibatani-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:4em"><div style="position:absolute;left:1em">b.</div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">アィヌ</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">Aynu</p><p style="margin: 0px;">person</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">エㇰ。</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ek.</p><p style="margin: 0px;">come</p></div><p style="display: none;">アィヌ エㇰ。</p><p style="display: none;">Aynu ek.</p><p style="display: none;">person come</p><p style="clear: left;">'A person came.'<sup id="cite_ref-Shibatani_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shibatani-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:4em"><div style="position:absolute;left:1em">c.</div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">ポン</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">Pon</p><p style="margin: 0px;">small</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">ツ゚レシ</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">turesi</p><p style="margin: 0px;">sister</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">カ</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ka</p><p style="margin: 0px;">too</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">イサㇺ。</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">isam.</p><p style="margin: 0px;">die</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;">（石狩）</p><p style="margin: 0px;">(Ishikari)</p><p style="margin: 0px;">&#160;</p></div><p style="display: none;">ポン ツ゚レシ カ イサㇺ。</p><p style="display: none;">Pon turesi ka isam.</p><p style="display: none;">small sister too die</p><p style="clear: left;">'The small sister too died.'<sup id="cite_ref-Shibatani_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shibatani-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transitive_and_ditransitive_sentences">Transitive and ditransitive sentences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Transitive and ditransitive sentences"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:4em"><div style="position:absolute;left:1em">a.</div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">アエコィキ。</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">A-e-koyki.</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="first person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">1SG</abbr>-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="second person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">2SG</abbr>-kill</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;">(虎杖)</p><p style="margin: 0px;">(Itadori)</p><p style="margin: 0px;">&#160;</p></div><p style="display: none;">アエコィキ。</p><p style="display: none;">A-e-koyki.</p><p style="display: none;">1SG-2SG-kill</p><p style="clear: left;">'I kill you.'<sup id="cite_ref-Shibatani_19-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shibatani-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:4em"><div style="position:absolute;left:1em">b.</div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">金田一</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">Kindaichi</p><p style="margin: 0px;">Kindaichi</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">殿</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">tono</p><p style="margin: 0px;">chief</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">ニㇱパ</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">nispa</p><p style="margin: 0px;">sir</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">クヌカㇻ。</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ku-nukar.</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="first person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">1SG</abbr>-see</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;">&#160;</p><p style="margin: 0px;">(Ishikari)</p><p style="margin: 0px;">&#160;</p></div><p style="display: none;">金田一 殿 ニㇱパ クヌカㇻ。</p><p style="display: none;">Kindaichi tono nispa ku-nukar.</p><p style="display: none;">Kindaichi chief sir 1SG-see</p><p style="clear: left;">'I met Mr. Kindaichi.'<sup id="cite_ref-Shibatani_19-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shibatani-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:4em"><div style="position:absolute;left:1em">c.</div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">カムィ</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">Kamuy</p><p style="margin: 0px;">bear</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">ウンマ</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">umma</p><p style="margin: 0px;">horse</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ain" style="margin: 0px;">ラィケ。</p><p lang="ain-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">rayke.</p><p style="margin: 0px;">kill</p></div><p style="display: none;">カムィ ウンマ ラィケ。</p><p style="display: none;">Kamuy umma rayke.</p><p style="display: none;">bear horse kill</p><p style="clear: left;">'A bear killed a horse.'<sup id="cite_ref-Shibatani_19-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shibatani-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing">Writing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Writing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Yoanne-Batchelor-01.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Yoanne-Batchelor-01.png/220px-Yoanne-Batchelor-01.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="378" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Yoanne-Batchelor-01.png/330px-Yoanne-Batchelor-01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Yoanne-Batchelor-01.png/440px-Yoanne-Batchelor-01.png 2x" data-file-width="443" data-file-height="761" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a> in Latin-script Ainu.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The Ainu language is written in a modified version of the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a> <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabary</a>, although it is possible for Japanese loan words and names to be written in <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a> (for example, "mobile phone" can be written <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language text"><span lang="ain">ケイタイデンワ</span></span> or <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language text"><span lang="ain">携帯電話</span></span>). There is also a <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin-based alphabet</a> in use. The <i><a href="/wiki/Ainu_Times" title="Ainu Times">Ainu Times</a></i> publishes in both. In the Latin <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ts/</span> is spelled <i>c</i> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/</span> is spelled <i>y</i>; the glottal stop, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʔ]</span>, which only occurs initially before accented vowels, is not written. Other phonemes use the same character as the IPA transcription given above. An equals sign (=) is used to mark <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a> boundaries, such as after a prefix. Its pitch accent is denoted by <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acute accent</a> in Latin script (e.g., <i>á</i>). This is usually not denoted in katakana.
</p><p>Rev. <a href="/wiki/John_Batchelor_(missionary)" title="John Batchelor (missionary)">John Batchelor</a> was an English missionary who lived among the Ainu, studied them and published many works on the Ainu language.<sup id="cite_ref-Frédéric_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frédéric-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Batchelor wrote extensively, both works about the Ainu language and works in Ainu itself. He was the first to write in Ainu and use a writing system for it.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Ainu" title="Bible translations into Ainu">Batchelor's translations</a> of various books of the Bible were published from 1887, and his <i><a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a></i> translation was published in Yokohama in 1897 by a joint committee of the <a href="/wiki/British_and_Foreign_Bible_Society" title="British and Foreign Bible Society">British and Foreign Bible Society</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Bible_Society" title="American Bible Society">American Bible Society</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Bible_Society" title="Scottish Bible Society">National Bible Society of Scotland</a>. Other books written in Ainu include dictionaries, a grammar, and books on Ainu culture and language.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_katakana_for_the_Ainu_language">Special katakana for the Ainu language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Special katakana for the Ainu language"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> standard exists for a set of extended katakana (<a href="/wiki/Katakana_Phonetic_Extensions" title="Katakana Phonetic Extensions">Katakana Phonetic Extensions</a>) for transliterating the Ainu language and other languages written with katakana.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> These characters are used to write final consonants and sounds that cannot be expressed using conventional katakana. The extended katakana are based on regular katakana and either are smaller in size or have a <a href="/wiki/Handakuten" class="mw-redirect" title="Handakuten">handakuten</a>. As few fonts yet support these extensions, workarounds exist for many of the characters, such as using a smaller font with the regular katakana <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ク</span></span> <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">ku</i></span> to produce <span style="font-size:70%;" lang="ja" class="t_nihongo_kana">ク</span> to represent the separate small katakana glyph <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language text"><span lang="ain">ㇰ</span></span> <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn">ku</i></span> used as in <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language text"><span lang="ain">アイヌイタㇰ</span></span> (<span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn">Ainu itak</i></span>).
</p><p>This is a list of special katakana used in transcribing the Ainu language. Most of the characters are of the extended set of katakana, though a few have been used historically in Japanese,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and thus are part of the main set of katakana. A number of previously proposed characters have not been added to Unicode as they can be represented as a sequence of two existing codepoints.
</p>

<td colspan="6"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-voicing-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-voicing_24-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-voicing_24-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-voicing_24-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-voicing_24-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>k</i>, <i>t</i>, <i>c</i>, <i>p</i> are sometimes voiced <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_plosive" title="Voiced velar plosive">ɡ</a>&#93;</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_plosive" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced alveolar plosive">d</a>&#93;</span>, [dz~dʒ], <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_bilabial_plosive" title="Voiced bilabial plosive">b</a>&#93;</span>, respectively. It does not change the meaning of a word, but it sounds more rough/masculine. When they are voiced, they may be written as <i>g</i>, <i>d</i>, <i>j</i>, <i>dz</i>, <i>b</i>, ガ, ダ, ヂャ, ヅァ, バ, etc.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-allograph-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-allograph_25-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-allograph_25-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-allograph_25-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-allograph_25-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-allograph_25-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-allograph_25-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-allograph_25-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-allograph_25-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-allograph_25-8"><sup><i><b>i</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-allograph_25-9"><sup><i><b>j</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-allograph_25-10"><sup><i><b>k</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Either may be used according to actual pronunciations, or to writer's preferred styles.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">ッ is final <i>t</i> at the end of a word (e.g. <i>pet</i> = ペッ = ペㇳ). In the middle of a polysyllabic word, it is a final consonant preceding the initial with a same value (e.g. <i>orta</i> /otta/ = オッタ; オㇿタ is not preferred).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The last sentence lacks clarity. Why is オッタ still romanized orta when all other sources uses otta? (December 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At the end of a word, <i>n</i> can be written either ㇴ or ン. In the middle of a polysyllabic word, it is ン. (e.g. <i>tan-mosir</i> = タンモシㇼ = タㇴ+モシㇼ, but not タㇴモシㇼ.)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_bilabial_nasal" title="Voiced bilabial nasal">m</a>&#93;</span> before <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_bilabial_plosive" title="Voiceless bilabial plosive">p</a>&#93;</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_nasal" title="Voiced velar nasal">ŋ</a>&#93;</span> before <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_plosive" title="Voiceless velar plosive">k</a>&#93;</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_nasal" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced alveolar nasal">n</a>&#93;</span> elsewhere. Unlike Japanese, it does not become other sounds such as <a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">nasal vowels</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-h-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-h_29-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-h_29-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Initial <i>h</i> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_glottal_fricative" title="Voiceless glottal fricative">h</a>&#93;</span> and final <i>h</i> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">x</a>&#93;</span> are different phonemes. Final <i>h</i> exists in Sakhalin Ainu only.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diphthongs">Diphthongs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Diphthongs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Syllable_coda" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllable coda">Final</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-front unrounded vowel">ɪ</a>&#93;</span> is spelled <i>y</i> in Latin, small ィ in katakana. Final <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-back_rounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-back rounded vowel">ʊ</a>&#93;</span> is spelled <i>w</i> in Latin, small ゥ in katakana. Large イ and ウ are used if there is a morpheme boundary with イ and ウ at the morpheme head. <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ae]</span> is spelled <i>ae</i>, アエ or アェ.
</p><p>Example with <a href="/wiki/Syllable_onset" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllable onset">initial</a> <i>k</i>:
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Oral_literature">Oral literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Oral literature"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Ainu have a rich <a href="/wiki/Oral_literature" title="Oral literature">oral tradition</a> of hero-sagas called <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Yukar" title="Yukar">yukar</a></i></span>, which retain a number of grammatical and lexical archaisms. <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn">Yukar</i></span> were memorized and told at get-togethers and ceremonies that often lasted hours or even days. The Ainu also have another form of narrative often used called <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Uepeker" title="Uepeker">Uepeker</a></i></span>, which was used in the same contexts.
</p><p>A native written form of the Ainu language has never existed; therefore, the Ainu people traditionally relied on memorization and oral communication to pass down their literature to the next generation.<sup id="cite_ref-NPMMp3_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPMMp3-30">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Ainu literature includes nonfiction, such as their history and "hunting adventures," and fiction such as stories about spiritual avatars, magic,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> myths, and heroes.<sup id="cite_ref-NPMMp3_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPMMp3-30">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_on_oral_literature">Research on oral literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Research on oral literature"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1134653256">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9;display:flow-root}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096940132">.mw-parser-output .listen .side-box-text{line-height:1.1em}.mw-parser-output .listen-plain{border:none;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded{width:100%;margin:0;border-width:1px 0 0 0;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-header{padding:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded .listen-header{padding:2px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen-file-header{padding:4px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen .description{padding-top:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen .mw-tmh-player{max-width:100%}@media(max-width:719px){.mw-parser-output .listen{clear:both}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .listen:not(.listen-noimage){width:320px}.mw-parser-output .listen-left{overflow:visible;float:left}.mw-parser-output .listen-center{float:none;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><figure class="mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></span><figcaption></figcaption></figure></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Audio_recording_of_an_Ainu_song_by_Bronis%C5%82aw_Pi%C5%82sudski.mp3" title="File:Audio recording of an Ainu song by Bronisław Piłsudski.mp3">Audio recording of an Ainu song by Bronisław Piłsudski</a></div>
<div><span typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="232" style="width:232px;" data-durationhint="21" data-mwtitle="Audio_recording_of_an_Ainu_song_by_Bronisław_Piłsudski.mp3" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/0/03/Audio_recording_of_an_Ainu_song_by_Bronis%C5%82aw_Pi%C5%82sudski.mp3/Audio_recording_of_an_Ainu_song_by_Bronis%C5%82aw_Pi%C5%82sudski.mp3.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/03/Audio_recording_of_an_Ainu_song_by_Bronis%C5%82aw_Pi%C5%82sudski.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></div>
<div class="description">An audio recording of an Ainu song by <a href="/wiki/Bronis%C5%82aw_Pi%C5%82sudski" title="Bronisław Piłsudski">Bronisław Piłsudski</a></div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>The oral literature of the Ainu languages has been studied mainly by Japanese and European researchers;<sup id="cite_ref-NPMMp4_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPMMp4-32">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> thus, Ainu literature has been transcribed using writing systems such as Japanese <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a> (commonly used for foreign-language text) and the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a>, and documented in the languages of the researchers themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> One prominent researcher of the Ainu languages is <a href="/wiki/Bronis%C5%82aw_Pi%C5%82sudski" title="Bronisław Piłsudski">Bronisław Piłsudski</a>, a Polish anthropologist who lived in Sakhalin from 1886 to 1905,<sup id="cite_ref-NPMMp4_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPMMp4-32">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and who published "Materials for the Study of the Ainu Language and Folklore" in 1912.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In addition, Piłsudski made audio recordings from 1902 to 1903, which is believed to be the first attempt to do so in the history of Ainu oral literature study.<sup id="cite_ref-NPMMp4_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPMMp4-32">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Japanese linguist Kyosuke Kindaichi is also famous for his work on the oral literature of the Ainu languages,<sup id="cite_ref-NPMMp4_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPMMp4-32">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and for his publication <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Ainu monogatari: tsuketari Ainugo taii oyobi goi</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">あいぬ物語: つけたりあいぬご たいい およびご</span></span>)</span> in 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history">Recent history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Recent history"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many of the speakers of Ainu lost the language with the advent of Japanese colonization, which formally began with the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Hokkaid%C5%8D_Development_Commission" title="Hokkaidō Development Commission">Hokkaido Colonization Office</a> in 1869. Japanese officials viewed the assimilation of Ainu a critical component of the Hokkaido colonization project, and developed policies designed to discourage or eliminate the use of the Ainu language, cultural practices, and traditional lifeways.<sup id="cite_ref-transition_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-transition-36">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maruyama_2014_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maruyama_2014-37">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-38">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The assimilation included the exploitation of Ainu land, the commodification of their culture, and the placing of Ainu children in schools where they learned only Japanese.<sup id="cite_ref-transition_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-transition-36">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maruyama_2014_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maruyama_2014-37">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-38">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>More recently, the Japanese government has acknowledged the Ainu people as an indigenous population. As of 1997 they were given indigenous rights under the United Nations Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples (UNDRIP) to their culture, heritage, and language.<sup id="cite_ref-transition_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-transition-36">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maruyama_2014_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maruyama_2014-37">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Savage_&amp;_Longo_2013_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Savage_&amp;_Longo_2013-39">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ainu Cultural Promotion Act in 1997 appointed the Foundation for Research and Promotion of Ainu Culture (FRPAC).<sup id="cite_ref-Savage_&amp;_Longo_2013_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Savage_&amp;_Longo_2013-39">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> This foundation is tasked with language education, where they promote Ainu language learning through training instructors, advanced language classes and creation and development of language materials.<sup id="cite_ref-Savage_&amp;_Longo_2013_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Savage_&amp;_Longo_2013-39">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Revitalization">Revitalization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Revitalization"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In general, Ainu people are hard to find because they tend to hide their identity as Ainu, especially in the young generation. Two thirds of Ainu youth do not know that they are Ainu.<sup id="cite_ref-gayman_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gayman-40">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In addition, because Ainu students were strongly discouraged from speaking their language at school,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> it has been challenging for the Ainu language to be revitalized.
</p><p>Despite this, there is an active movement to <a href="/wiki/Language_revitalization" title="Language revitalization">revitalize</a> the language, mainly in Hokkaido but also elsewhere such as <a href="/wiki/Kant%C5%8D_region" title="Kantō region">Kanto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin2011_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin2011-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Ainu oral literature has been documented both in hopes of safeguarding it for future generations, as well as using it as a teaching tool for language learners.<sup id="cite_ref-Miyaoka,_Sakiyama,_Krauss_2007_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miyaoka,_Sakiyama,_Krauss_2007-42">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Beginning in 1987, the <a href="/wiki/Ainu_Association_of_Hokkaido" title="Ainu Association of Hokkaido">Ainu Association of Hokkaido</a> with approximately 500 members<sup id="cite_ref-Martin2011_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin2011-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> began hosting 14 Ainu language classes, Ainu language instructors training courses and Family Ainu Learning Initiative<sup id="cite_ref-gayman_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gayman-40">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and have released instructional materials on the language, including a textbook.<sup id="cite_ref-Miyaoka,_Sakiyama,_Krauss_2007_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miyaoka,_Sakiyama,_Krauss_2007-42">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Also, <a href="/wiki/Yamato_people" title="Yamato people">Yamato</a> linguists teach Ainu and train students to become Ainu instructors in university.<sup id="cite_ref-gayman_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gayman-40">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In spite of these efforts, as of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> the Ainu language was not yet taught as a subject in any secondary school in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin2011_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin2011-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the Ainu Cultural Promotion Act of 1997, Ainu dictionaries transformed and became tools for improving communication and preserving records of the Ainu language in order to revitalize the language and promote the culture.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> This act had aims to promote, disseminate, and advocate on behalf of Ainu cultural traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The main issue with this act however, was that not a single Ainu person was included in the "Expert" meetings prior to the law's passage, and as a result of this there was no mention of language education and how it should be carried out.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The focus at this point was on Ainu culture revitalization rather than Ainu language revitalization.
</p><p>As of 2011, there has been an increasing number of second-language learners, especially in Hokkaido, in large part due to the pioneering efforts of the late Ainu folklorist, activist and former <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Diet" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese Diet">Diet</a> member <a href="/wiki/Shigeru_Kayano" class="mw-redirect" title="Shigeru Kayano">Shigeru Kayano</a>, himself a native speaker, who first opened an Ainu language school in 1987 funded by <a href="/wiki/Ainu_Kyokai" class="mw-redirect" title="Ainu Kyokai">Ainu Kyokai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Teeter_&amp;_Okazaki_2011_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Teeter_&amp;_Okazaki_2011-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ainu_Association_of_Hokkaido" title="Ainu Association of Hokkaido">Ainu Association of Hokkaido</a> is the main supporter of Ainu culture in Hokkaido.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin2011_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin2011-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Ainu language classes have been conducted in some areas in Japan and small numbers of young people are learning Ainu. Efforts have also been made to produce web-accessible materials for conversational Ainu because most documentation of the Ainu language focused on the recording of folktales.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The Ainu language has been in media as well; the first Ainu radio program was called <i>FM Pipaushi</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> which has run since 2001 along with 15-minute radio Ainu language lessons funded by FRPAC,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Ainu_Times" title="Ainu Times">The Ainu Times</a></i> has been established since 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-Teeter_&amp;_Okazaki_2011_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Teeter_&amp;_Okazaki_2011-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, a radio course was broadcast by the STVradio Broadcasting to introduce Ainu language. The course put extensive efforts in promoting the language, creating 4 text books in each season throughout the year.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, the Ainu language has been seen in public domains such as the outlet shopping complex's name, <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn">Rera</i></span>, which means 'wind', in the <a href="/wiki/Minami-Chitose_Station" title="Minami-Chitose Station">Minami Chitose</a> area and the name <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn">Pewre</i></span>, meaning 'young', at a shopping centre in the <a href="/wiki/Chitose,_Hokkaido" title="Chitose, Hokkaido">Chitose</a> area. There is also a basketball team in <a href="/wiki/Sapporo" title="Sapporo">Sapporo</a> founded under the name <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn">Rera Kamuy Hokkaido</i></span>, after <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn">rera kamuy</i></span> 'god of the wind' (its current name is <a href="/wiki/Levanga_Hokkaido" title="Levanga Hokkaido">Levanga Hokkaido</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Martin2011_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin2011-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The well-known Japanese fashion magazine's name <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Non-no" title="Non-no">Non-no</a></i></span> means 'flower' in Ainu.
</p><p>Another Ainu language revitalization program is Urespa, a university program to educate high-level persons on the language of the Ainu. The effort is a collaborative and cooperative program for individuals wishing to learn about Ainu languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Uzawa_168–179_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uzawa_168–179-50">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> This includes performances which focus on the Ainu and their language, instead of using the dominant Japanese language.<sup id="cite_ref-Uzawa_168–179_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uzawa_168–179-50">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another form of Ainu language revitalization is an annual national competition, which is Ainu language-themed. People of many differing demographics are often encouraged to take part in the contest. Since 2017, the popularity of the contest has increased.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ainu language has also been featured in the manga and anime <i><a href="/wiki/Golden_Kamuy" title="Golden Kamuy">Golden Kamuy</a></i>.
</p><p>On 15 February 2019, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> approved a bill to recognize the Ainu language for the first time<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and enacted the law on April 19, 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside of Japan, there have also been efforts to revive the Ainu culture and language in other countries, including <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, researchers working together from both the Society for Academic Research of Ainu (SARC), representatives from Hokkaido University, and with the assistance of linguists spanning multiple universities and countries assisted in the creation of AI Pirika, an AI created with the goal of assisting with speech recognition and serving as a conversation partner.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_text">Sample text</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ainu_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Sample text"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Below is a sample text from a traditional Ainu folktale, in Ainu, Japanese and English.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Ainu original
</th>
<th>Latin transliteration
</th>
<th>Japanese translation
</th>
<th>English translation
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Ainu (Japan)-language text"><span lang="ain">シネアン ト  タ ベテトㇰ ウン シノタㇱ クス パイェアㇱ アワ, ベテトㇰタ シネ ポンルㇷ゚ネクㇿ ネスコ ウライ カㇿ クス ウライキㇰ ネアㇷ゚ コサニㇰケ ウカン プナㇱ・プナㇱ。</span></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn">Sinean to ta petetok un sinotas kusu payeas awa, petetokta sine ponrupnekur nesko urai kar kusu uraikik neap kosanikkeukan punas-punas.</i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">ある日に（川の）水源の方へ（私が）遊ぶに（私が）出かけたら（思いがけなく）（川の）水源に一人の小男が胡桃（くるみ）の木の梁（やな）をたてるため（胡桃の木の）杭を打っていた。 （それに下げた）腰を幾度も上げて立っている。(腰を曲げ曲げしている。)</span></span>
</td>
<td>One day, as I went out to play at the spring, there was a little man at the spring hitting stakes made of walnut wood, in order to erect (some) wooden beams. His hip bent (and he bent down) and he straightened out (as he worked, going up and down over and over).
</td></tr></tbody></table>

<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFIvar_Lissner1957" class="citation book cs1">Ivar Lissner (1957). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=0skIAQAAIAAJ&amp;q=1877+theologian+john+batchelor++missionary+studied"><i>The Living Past</i></a> (4&#160;ed.). Putnam's. p.&#160;204<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">23 April</span> 2012</span>. <q>In 1877 a young and industrious theologian went to visit the Ainu. His name was John Batchelor, and he was a scientist and missionary. He got to know the Ainu well, studied their language and customs, won their affection, and remained their staunch friend until the end of his days. It is to Batchelor that we owe our deepest insight into the</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Living+Past&amp;rft.pages=204&amp;rft.edition=4&amp;rft.pub=Putnam%27s&amp;rft.date=1957&amp;rft.au=Ivar+Lissner&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D0skIAQAAIAAJ%26q%3D1877%2Btheologian%2Bjohn%2Bbatchelor%2B%2Bmissionary%2Bstudied&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAinu+language" class="Z3988"></span> [Original from the University of California Digitized Jan 27, 2009 Length 444 pages]</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFPatric1943" class="citation book cs1">Patric, John (1943). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=wbFCAAAAIAAJ&amp;q=batchelor+ainu+hitherto+wrote+books"><i>...Why Japan Was Strong</i></a> (4&#160;ed.). Doubleday, Doran &amp; Company. p.&#160;72<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">23 April</span> 2012</span>. <q>John Batchelor set about to learn the Ainu language, which the Japanese had not troubled ever to learn. He laboriously compiled an Ainu dictionary. He singlehandedly turned this hitherto but spoken tongue into a written language, and himself wrote books in it.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=...Why+Japan+Was+Strong&amp;rft.pages=72&amp;rft.edition=4&amp;rft.pub=Doubleday%2C+Doran+%26+Company&amp;rft.date=1943&amp;rft.aulast=Patric&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DwbFCAAAAIAAJ%26q%3Dbatchelor%2Bainu%2Bhitherto%2Bwrote%2Bbooks&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAinu+language" class="Z3988"></span> [Original from the University of California Digitized Oct 16, 2007 Length 313 pages]</span>
</li>
